    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer5897249232524673519">"Il existe des êtres qui ne subissent pas la loi de l'évolution. Ce sont les légumes cliniques", ou des tubes par où circule seule la nourriture. Ces tubes ne sont pas pour autant sans cervelle puisqu'il arrive que celle-ci, suite à un "accident fatal", se réveille soudain, et déclenche la vie. C'est exactement ce qu'a vécu la (très) jeune narratrice de Métaphysique des tu</span>
  <span id="freeText5897249232524673519" style="display:none">"Il existe des êtres qui ne subissent pas la loi de l'évolution. Ce sont les légumes cliniques", ou des tubes par où circule seule la nourriture. Ces tubes ne sont pas pour autant sans cervelle puisqu'il arrive que celle-ci, suite à un "accident fatal", se réveille soudain, et déclenche la vie. C'est exactement ce qu'a vécu la (très) jeune narratrice de Métaphysique des tubes durant les deux premières années de sa vie qui furent muettes, immobiles, végétatives, bref divines. Au sens propre, car ce singulier bébé n'ignore pas qu'il est Dieu lui-même, méditant sur ce monde qu'il hésite à rejoindre. Sous forme de monologues intérieurs, considérations philosophico-drolatiques, on déguste le récit de ces trois premières années d'une vie française au Japon, pays merveilleux où de la naissance à la maternelle, l'enfant est un dieu.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5897249232524673519" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>

<div id="review_129657101" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/129657101" />
    <a title="Petra-X" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1237196-petra-x"><img alt="Petra-X" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603179398p2/1237196.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/129657101?book_show_action=true">Nov 07, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Petra-X" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Petra-X" href="/user/show/1237196-petra-x">Petra-X</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780571220496" href="/book/show/71457.The_Character_of_Rain">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1237196-petra-x?shelf=travel-adventure-countries">travel-adventure-countries</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1237196-petra-x?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer129657101&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer129657101" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2334511733472985095">This is a book about babyhood and childhood told as if an adult could interpret how a very small child actually thinks, but also giving the child the adult abilities of talking and reading. <br/><br/>I loved the book, not least because it was suitably short, but it turns out that her one, not quite phobia, but absolute real dislike, is also mine, and for the same reason too. I intensely dislike koi, or carp, waiting to be fed with their mouths open and being able to see their disgusting rubbery lips and t</span>
  <span id="freeText2334511733472985095" style="display:none">This is a book about babyhood and childhood told as if an adult could interpret how a very small child actually thinks, but also giving the child the adult abilities of talking and reading. <br /><br />I loved the book, not least because it was suitably short, but it turns out that her one, not quite phobia, but absolute real dislike, is also mine, and for the same reason too. I intensely dislike koi, or carp, waiting to be fed with their mouths open and being able to see their disgusting rubbery lips and the smooth pink tube that is their mouth cavity and digestive tract tube. <br /><br />I think both the protagonist and myself had the same feeling on feeding them for the first time where they crowd up, pushing pushing, to the side of the pond and in their mindless, greedy way open their maws for the crumbs thrown at them. Intimations of our own mortality indeed, wide open mouths and a tube of throat and gullet straight to our stomachs.<br /><br />Good book though. Worth reading because its nothing like any other book I've ever read and the writing is sensitive and lovely and never belabours a point. Unlike me. So enough.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2334511733472985095" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_346830153" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/346830153" />
    <a title="Cecily" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1199525-cecily"><img alt="Cecily" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1480285828p2/1199525.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/346830153?book_show_action=true">Jun 11, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cecily" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cecily" href="/user/show/1199525-cecily">Cecily</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780571220496" href="/book/show/71457.The_Character_of_Rain">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1199525-cecily?shelf=biog-and-autobiog">biog-and-autobiog</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1199525-cecily?shelf=china-japan-asia">china-japan-asia</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer346830153" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2614388120688707076">Really 2.5*: it's not a bad book, but I didn't really enjoy it (fortunately, it's very short). <br/><br/>This describes Nothomb's life from her birth until her third birthday. The youngest child of a Belgian diplomat in Japan, she tells it with the Japanese assumption that children are gods until their third birthday: "at three... you see everything and understand nothing" because you don't remember the previous year (though she claims to). Unfortunately, I quickly found the gimmick of the omniscient and </span>
  <span id="freeText2614388120688707076" style="display:none">Really 2.5*: it's not a bad book, but I didn't really enjoy it (fortunately, it's very short). <br /><br />This describes Nothomb's life from her birth until her third birthday. The youngest child of a Belgian diplomat in Japan, she tells it with the Japanese assumption that children are gods until their third birthday: "at three... you see everything and understand nothing" because you don't remember the previous year (though she claims to). Unfortunately, I quickly found the gimmick of the omniscient and self-centred narrator rather annoying. <br /><br />For the first two years, she is totally unresponsive and silent and is referred to as the Tube (feeding) and the Plant. Then she suddenly switches on and screams almost constantly for six months. She is calmed by her first taste of chocolate, and from then on remembers everything, observing "There isn't any point remembering that which has no connection to pleasure" - the sort of idea that is meant to be profound, but is actually fatuous. She becomes a model child, "The very idea of pleasure had set me into motion".<br /><br />After that, even allowing for hindsight, she claims far more knowledge and intelligence than is remotely possible, e.g. pondering long and hard about which of the many words she had secretly learned would be the first, second and third that she uttered, weighing up the impact of each. She also taught herself to read before the age of three without anyone else realising. I'm not sure if this is meant to be serious, cute, funny or something else. She admits she "stands little chance of being believed. I don't particularly care". What a charming way for an author to address her readers.<br /><br />The idea of being a god makes her insufferable: one of her nannies adores her, but the other detests her: "How terrible it must have been for her not to adore me"! I presume this is meant to be amusing, but by this point I'd already taken a dislike to her, so it merely confirmed my negative feelings. <br /><br />Then, amidst such annoying writing is the odd gem: "When I finally reached the water [the sea], it turned out to be as afraid of me as I was of it. Like a timid child, it approached and then ran away. I did the same."<br /><br />Unsurprisingly, turning three and no longer being thought of as a god was not a happy experience. "Turning three brought absolutely nothing good with it", and unpleasant realisations such as "eventually we lose what we love. That which is given you will be taken back" and "One of the most terrifying laws of the universe: what doesn't advance retreats, First comes growth and then decay, and between the two is a void". Frankly, Amelie, I don't give a damn. <br /><br />The next stage of Nothomb's life, in a diplomatic compound in Peking, is described in slightly more realistic terms in Loving Sabotage: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/342140589">http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/...</a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2614388120688707076" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer3766161931047429331">A book narrated by a two-year-old intellectual prodigy, slyly based on the author’s own upbringing in Japan. As the blurb states, the book looks at the Japanese notion of <i>okosama</i>, or the Lord Child, a piece of lore where children are revered as Gods until they are three. This is true of a toddler’s own outlook: there is no one more important in the world than themselves—attention lavished on others is downright insulting.<br/><br/>What transpires is a curious novel about a two-year-old experiencing the wo</span>
  <span id="freeText3766161931047429331" style="display:none">A book narrated by a two-year-old intellectual prodigy, slyly based on the author’s own upbringing in Japan. As the blurb states, the book looks at the Japanese notion of <i>okosama</i>, or the Lord Child, a piece of lore where children are revered as Gods until they are three. This is true of a toddler’s own outlook: there is no one more important in the world than themselves—attention lavished on others is downright insulting.<br /><br />What transpires is a curious novel about a two-year-old experiencing the world through her distended brain. Imagine a more compassionate (and female) Stewie Griffin in a posh Japanese house, with a father proficient in Noh singing and a bitter ex-aristocrat nanny who leaves her to die. This is fun and a little more cerebral than the satire of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/933802.Acide_sulfurique" title="Acide sulfurique by Amélie Nothomb" rel="nofollow noopener">Sulphuric Acid</a> or the wry storytelling of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1003801.The_Book_of_Proper_Names_A_Novel" title="The Book of Proper Names A Novel by Amélie Nothomb" rel="nofollow noopener">The Book of Proper Names</a>. Worth a snifter for the uninitiated. </span>
  <a data-text-id="3766161931047429331" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_767009131" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/767009131" />
    <a title="Lukasz Pruski" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2989615-lukasz-pruski"><img alt="Lukasz Pruski" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1362764033p2/2989615.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/767009131?book_show_action=true">Nov 16, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lukasz Pruski" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lukasz Pruski" href="/user/show/2989615-lukasz-pruski">Lukasz Pruski</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780312286002" href="/book/show/1939790.The_Character_of_Rain">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer767009131" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6572707913269694026">Writers create worlds in their books. These worlds may be reflections of the real, physical world, but they do not have to be. The only constraint writers are subject to while creating these worlds is to make them internally consistent (and original and captivating, of course). Amelie Nothomb has created in her books a world in which a little child (in "The Character of Rain" the narrator is Amelie herself at the age between two and three) is fully developed intellectually and emotionally, able </span>
  <span id="freeText6572707913269694026" style="display:none">Writers create worlds in their books. These worlds may be reflections of the real, physical world, but they do not have to be. The only constraint writers are subject to while creating these worlds is to make them internally consistent (and original and captivating, of course). Amelie Nothomb has created in her books a world in which a little child (in "The Character of Rain" the narrator is Amelie herself at the age between two and three) is fully developed intellectually and emotionally, able to manipulates her parents, reads, and speaks two languages. Of course it is not in any way a reflection of reality, but the world Ms. Nothomb has created is wonderfully consistent and provides a unique point of view for observations of the outside world (the real one - meaning the silly and absurd one).<br /><br />It is hard not to love the statement "humanity has formed a cult about normalcy". Amelie certainly is not into normalcy. Normal people are born when they are 0 days old. At that age Amelie was just transformed from being a tube into being a God. Her proper birth happened "at the age of two and a half" and she owes the event to her Belgian grandmother or rather to Belgian white chocolate. <br /><br />To me, "The Character of Rain" is not as powerful a study of childhood as "Loving Sabotage" by the same author, but it is a very good book nevertheless, full of whimsy, wisdom, and humor. It has a few less captivating fragments, but the good parts dominate. There are hilarious bits (for instance, how Amelie names the three carp or how she helps her father during very heavy rain), but there are also moments of great depth. Ms. Nothomb attempts to show how an "it" transforms into an "I" - how the human identity is formed. The sweet and sad thread where Amelie realizes that things are not permanent is deeply moving. Add to that vivid portrayal of some aspects of Japanese culture (as Amelie is born - in both ways - in Japan).<br /><br />No publisher in the U.S. would attempt to sell a book with the original French title "Metaphysics of Tubes" (<i>Metaphysique des Tubes</i>), so the novel has been retitled "The Character of Rain". I was not happy about the change because the concept of "tubeness" is alluded to at various places and it resonates with me deeply (for personal reasons), but I have to admit that the alternative title is well grounded in the book.<br />.<br />Had I not read "Loving Sabotage" earlier, I would have rated this novel with five stars. Sadly, the four and a half is now rounded down to four.<br /><br />Four and a half stars.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6572707913269694026" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review767009131">

<div id="review_198836740" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/198836740" />
    <a title="Nick Klagge" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2704424-nick-klagge"><img alt="Nick Klagge" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1355921488p2/2704424.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/198836740?book_show_action=true">Aug 15, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nick Klagge" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nick Klagge" href="/user/show/2704424-nick-klagge">Nick Klagge</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780312286002" href="/book/show/1939790.The_Character_of_Rain">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer198836740" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11317008496214656921">I found reading this book to be a pretty unpleasant experience--surprising, as it was recommended by a good friend of mine. <br/><br/>The narrator is a child prodigy who goes from age 0 to age 3 in this book. I found her extremely unpleasant, and two main touchstones came to mind. One was Alia Atreides from the "Dune" novels, who is called "The Abomination" because she is born with the memories and mental capacities of an adult. The sense in which this is an "abomination" was left mostly tacit in the Dune</span>
  <span id="freeText11317008496214656921" style="display:none">I found reading this book to be a pretty unpleasant experience--surprising, as it was recommended by a good friend of mine. <br /><br />The narrator is a child prodigy who goes from age 0 to age 3 in this book. I found her extremely unpleasant, and two main touchstones came to mind. One was Alia Atreides from the "Dune" novels, who is called "The Abomination" because she is born with the memories and mental capacities of an adult. The sense in which this is an "abomination" was left mostly tacit in the Dune novels, but is illustrated in great detail. There is something unnerving and fundamentally problematic about a child with adult thought processes. The second touchstone was Humbert Humbert, from "Lolita." The narrator of CoR is just like HH in her view of other people as wholly instrumental and subordinated to her own designs. She has a baroque fondness for sensual pleasures and for ego-stroking, but not for the happiness that comes from authentic relationships. It's possible that this was an intent of the author, but I also found a second-order unpleasantness in the sense that I was rooting for bad things to happen to a 2-year-old.<br /><br />I think it's very possible to read this book as an (unintended) illustration of the problems of self-fashioning associated with Facebook and Web 2.0. (This perspective is largely informed by my reading of the blog "Marginal Utility Annex" by Rob Horning.) Although these specific tools play no role in the story, the narrator subjects herself to the same sort of reflexive subjectivity that Horning calls out as a problem with Web 2.0--rather than simply living her life, the narrator is obsessed with making deliberate choices about her actions to have specific effects on others--most obviously, her deep concern over the question of what her first words would be. (Analogously, e.g., "What bands should I put as my Favorite Music on Facebook?")<br /><br />I would have stopped reading this book, except that it was only 130 pages long and recommended by a close friend.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11317008496214656921" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Irma" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Irma" href="/user/show/2224021-irma">Irma</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer14189665854943025628">What a book! I was doubtful going into this, which then turned into wary, which slowly transformed into interested, then amused, then laughing out loud for a whole chapter (how the dad got into Noh! Brilliant stuff), and finally fascinated. All of this in 157 pages! <br/><br/>This is a book about a baby who spends 2 and a half years in a vegetative state. After two and a half years, she wakes up. The book describes the first three years of her life, including some of the time she was a "vegetable". As she</span>
  <span id="freeText14189665854943025628" style="display:none">What a book! I was doubtful going into this, which then turned into wary, which slowly transformed into interested, then amused, then laughing out loud for a whole chapter (how the dad got into Noh! Brilliant stuff), and finally fascinated. All of this in 157 pages! <br /><br />This is a book about a baby who spends 2 and a half years in a vegetative state. After two and a half years, she wakes up. The book describes the first three years of her life, including some of the time she was a "vegetable". As she says herself, her parents had three children: a boy, a girl, and a plant.<br /><br />I know this sounds super depressing and why would anyone want to read such a thing but it's GREAT. Very few bits are heavy and most of the book is just really interesting because it depicts the world through the eyes of a two/three-year-old. It's written kind of like an autobiography (first person, Nothomb really did live in Japan as a child) but it's fiction. Not sure if either is a problem for anyone but it's just a lovely read either way.<br /><br />Hvala KK za poklon. :D</span>

<div id="review_229511425" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/229511425" />
    <a title="Darius" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4588966-darius"><img alt="Darius" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1308864438p2/4588966.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/229511425?book_show_action=true">Oct 31, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Darius" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Darius" href="/user/show/4588966-darius">Darius</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780571220496" href="/book/show/71457.The_Character_of_Rain">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4588966-darius?shelf=fiction-belgium">fiction-belgium</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer229511425" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6468521184553285443">This is a difficult book to describe. Put briefly, the novel attempts to capture the mind of an infant. It is occasionally engaging and sometimes witty, but is spoiled by numerous pseudo-philosophical musings. These supposed pearls of wisdom include the following:<br/><br/>"There isn't any point to remembering that which has no connection to pleasure". Really? No point in remembering your past mistakes, then?<br/><br/>"For children the only true pleasure lies in eating". Obviously no child enjoys playing in the par</span>
  <span id="freeText6468521184553285443" style="display:none">This is a difficult book to describe. Put briefly, the novel attempts to capture the mind of an infant. It is occasionally engaging and sometimes witty, but is spoiled by numerous pseudo-philosophical musings. These supposed pearls of wisdom include the following:<br /><br />"There isn't any point to remembering that which has no connection to pleasure". Really? No point in remembering your past mistakes, then?<br /><br />"For children the only true pleasure lies in eating". Obviously no child enjoys playing in the park, or splashing water in the bath.<br /><br />"Thought isn't possible without language". Really? This isn't an absurd idea, but highly contentious nonetheless. Philosophers have been debating the relation of language to thought for centuries. <br /><br />"Seeing involves choice. Whoever looks at something has decided to fix his attention on that one thing, to the exclusion of other things. That is why sight, the very essence of life, constitutes a rejection". Why is sight "the very essence of life"? What is she talking about?<br /><br />"Knowing what will happen in the future, we are faced with a simple choice: either we resolve not to become attached to people and things, or we decide to love them even more fiercely". Or we love them in the same way as before.<br /><br />"I had begun to think that our individuality lay in the following: tell me what disgusts you and I will tell you who you are. Our personalities mean nothing; our inclinations are mostly ordinary. What disgusts us expresses who we really are." What utter bilge!<br /><br />"At three, you don't automatically ask adults questions, for you're not yet convinced that they have more wisdom. Perhaps that's not wrong." Perhaps it is. What three-year-old doesn't constantly ask "why"? Who seriously believes that an adult doesn't have more wisdom than a three-year-old?<br /><br />All this portentous nonsense is a pity, because there are many amusing episodes in this little novel. The account of the narrator's father studying under a Noh master is a finely crafted piece of comic writing. There are also some genuine insights in addition to the phony philosophy. Nothomb's description of a toddler's anguish when told that she will leave behind her beloved Japanese nurse is psychologically convincing.<br /><br />It takes more than charm, however, to mask this novel's more serious underlying failings. The few drops of genuine insight are lost in a sea of pretentious waffle.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6468521184553285443" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1591154104" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1591154104" />
    <a title="Lisa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9874631-lisa"><img alt="Lisa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1402496241p2/9874631.jpg" /></a>

<div id="review_3114894880" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3114894880" />
    <a title="Maryam" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/36694982-maryam"><img alt="Maryam" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1604685432p2/36694982.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3114894880?book_show_action=true">Jan 06, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Maryam" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Maryam" href="/user/show/36694982-maryam">Maryam</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780312302481" href="/book/show/820897.The_Character_of_Rain">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36694982-maryam?shelf=international">international</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3114894880" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17815432309557618372">“People are often asked what, as children, they wanted to be when they grew up. In my case it would be better to ask my parents. Their replies would provide an idea of precisely what I didn’t want to be when I grew up.”<br/><br/>As a kid, I’ve always wanted to be a scientist, not necessarily having a specific specialization in mind, but since I secretly liked insects, I was gradually fantasizing about becoming an entomologist as a grown up. I was also fond of astronomy and had the urge to learn more about</span>
  <span id="freeText17815432309557618372" style="display:none">“People are often asked what, as children, they wanted to be when they grew up. In my case it would be better to ask my parents. Their replies would provide an idea of precisely what I didn’t want to be when I grew up.”<br /><br />As a kid, I’ve always wanted to be a scientist, not necessarily having a specific specialization in mind, but since I secretly liked insects, I was gradually fantasizing about becoming an entomologist as a grown up. I was also fond of astronomy and had the urge to learn more about it without becoming an astronaut. The one pathetic urge that I was imposed on expressing (publicly but temporarily, thankfully") was linked to the “ common” idea of the well-recognized professions including medicine. <br />I’ve never liked medicine or any relatable fields. Yet, I was considering trying it out to please my parents. Regardless, I hated this idea of becoming sth in order to earn social recognition and esteem. I ended up trying out multiple majors such as IT, Engineering, and Biology in different universities. <br />It seemed that I couldn’t find myself in any of these arenas. <br />That’s when I finally decided to study whatever is there  Anyhow, everything seems repetitive and meaningless so to hell with it. This was developed into a collective indifference of the whole educational experience ( in institutionalized terms), which is meant to be practiced to offer ppl social acceptance and success in the long run.<br /> I don’t really know why we are supposed to answer the questions of “becoming” during childhood or even adulthood. It’s a matter of constant exploration. SELF-exploration, which is entirely detached from other individuals who might intervene in this process causing an inner confusion that can cut off ur damn journey to build it upon their unsettled emotions and experiences. <br />I know that I’m just another person studying the same nonsensical field as everyone else, but I’m somehow feeling relieved becz I had my shitty path to follow, not someone else’s. <br />I've graduated and got my diploma, a very boring paper that means literally nothing to me!<br />I didn't attend the commencement as it was planned from the very first moment I entered the Uni. <br /> I've always wanted to learn more, I love the fact that we don't know many things but at the same time, we can learn more and more about us and the universe by observing everything around us.. <br />listening, reading, sensing, thinking, growing, falling, and suffering. Those live experiences mean everything! <br />This is not found in universities or schools. Those places have some kind of a duty to be accomplished. Certain rules are constantly enforced and these institutions are supposed to produce copies of the copies.. <br />Knowledge is being supervised. <br />What higher institutions offer is a good opportunity for you to be another product. <br />I must admit that it can open up many doors to you even if you are not actually worthy of this opportunity. <br />Most students would take this advantage and become the product. All of us do that eventually.. by following different paths. <br />I might end up getting a master's or Phd.. this might sound contradictory.. but it wouldn’t mean that I'll be proud of what I’ve done. <br />These are all lousy attempts to be someone" <br />Almost everything we do is just attached to this social status that we think it must be fed.. <br /> <br /><br /><br />“You think you’re different? You’re a tube who emerged from a tube. Lately you’ve had some grand sense of being different, of having greater thoughts. Fool. You’re a tube and a tube is all you will ever be”<br /><br />Amèlie, you can use whatever language you want along the way of translating ur thoughts into living entities, even if you decided to choose none, and I would still be captivated by ur phantasmagoric shade of imagination. This shadow that you extract from your floating skies to pour it into your static cloud is the ultimate ecstasy that one can reach in the process of drawing magical dimensions with no boundaries chaining our physicality. Having a previous experience with your art( won’t say books or work), it feels like there are limitless visions are yet to be explored every time a traveler enters your surreal world. <br />Turning to the concrete (dull) part where I’m no longer talking to Nothomb in direct manner, this book is not only a bibliography of the author herself. It’s a spectrum of emotional, mental,ethereal, linguistic and philosophical insights all blended together to extrude an artistic image of the internal surface representing the essence of being. <br />Everything discussed starting from birth, curiosity, observational development, the brink of the cliff, to the realization of this ongoing process, and the void between these moments connects the basis of what we know as existence. Though the foundation of each world is interconnected ( the “so-called reality” and Amèlie’s private universe”), the nature of Amèlie’s world draws a phantom reality that penetrates what’s beneath your bones without leaving any trace behind. This is not exclusively applied to this particular book, ( okay, it’s called a book now becz “ again” I’m talking in a more descriptive manner!) This theme of everlasting peculiarity applies to all her work. And I’ve been so deeply in love with it ever since i invaded Amèlie’s world. <br />One incident that took place throughout the story is the fall of Amèlie’s father into a drain. <br />It reminded me of a similar experience with a reversal roles I’ve been through when i was a kid. <br />One day, i was walking with my father in the middle of nowhere. Suddenly, i ceased to be a walking body as i fell into a huge hole where i become nearly a crops. My father, not aware of wt was happening, kept walking until he realized (after some time) that he was walking alone! I laugh so hard every time i remember this cartoonish fall though my father and those who joined later to help getting me out took it very srsly. This fall in the story( the father’s metaphorical fall) represents the protagonist gradual awareness of her surroundings and the role of the concrete walls of reality in shaping our understanding of what and who we are compared to the abstract representation. At any rate, It was a very intriguing experience and i could relate to (the plant) the protagonist not only in this matter, but also in many existential /fictional moments expressed throughout the creation of time guided by Amèlie’s transparent watch combined by mine.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17815432309557618372" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer6594287540904276409">French is not my first language, nor my second. So the first times I started reading this I never got past page 17. For many months I ignored the book. Then a week ago I took it with me on the train and when I got home again I had reached page 57. <br/><br/>This is an amazing book, written from the point of view of a child between two-and-a-half and three years old, except for the first part of the book where the same child has a rather strange start in life which is told in third person (but still as muc</span>
  <span id="freeText6594287540904276409" style="display:none">French is not my first language, nor my second. So the first times I started reading this I never got past page 17. For many months I ignored the book. Then a week ago I took it with me on the train and when I got home again I had reached page 57. <br /><br />This is an amazing book, written from the point of view of a child between two-and-a-half and three years old, except for the first part of the book where the same child has a rather strange start in life which is told in third person (but still as much from the child as imaginable). I bored my family by sharing the thoughts of the child who feels she is the center of the universe and who at the same time has to invent this universe because she has no experience of it yet. That is why the book ends shortly after the third birthday: at three years old, one is no longer the center of the universe. Or as the author puts it: subsequently nothing more happened (which is probably not the best translation, English too is not my first language).<br /><br />I did not bother to look up all the words I didn't know, because that way I would probably have left the book unfinished again. But the parts where I did look them up I much enjoyed the language. I shall read it again when my French has improved.<br /><br /> </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer17759847914837751629">A more detailed description of Amelie's childhood. Like all her autobiographical works, I loved it!<br/><br/>The beginning was a most awkward and fascinating one. She awakes at the taste of chocolate, oh Amelie, I think I understand what you are trying to say.   <span id="freeText17759847914837751629" style="display:none">A more detailed description of Amelie's childhood. Like all her autobiographical works, I loved it!<br /><br />The beginning was a most awkward and fascinating one. She awakes at the taste of chocolate, oh Amelie, I think I understand what you are trying to say. Chocolate is a most exquisite food, is it not? :)<br /><br />When the "tube" finally awakens we get to see her experience in the beautiful Japan. I do think I loved Japan more through her eye. Every-time I hear about this country I fall in love again, they are the most fascinating people with a most unusual combination of all cultures, creating their own special something that can be described only as japanese. Of course, this book talks little about modern Japan, more about that in her other books: Fear and Trembling and Tokyo Fiancee. Here she is young and glimpses more of the traditional japan.<br /><br />Her suicidal nature was unexpected, why would a child want to kill herself? This author experiences the most unusual feelings as a child, still, I can't get enough of her descriptions. <br /><br />A very interesting book, but then again, most of hers are :) .</span>

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer54085252" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10782401206538257183">I have to say, Nothomb's books fascinate me. It's best not to read them as memoirs, though she draws from her life, as she freely admits she loves to lie and doesn't care if she's believed. That said, this is the story of a child/God/tube from day one to shortly after her third birthday. From scrupulously debating within herself what should be her third spoken word to finally confessing to her parents she knows how to speak (complex thoughts in complex sentences), Nothomb's little creation bears</span>
  <span id="freeText10782401206538257183" style="display:none">I have to say, Nothomb's books fascinate me. It's best not to read them as memoirs, though she draws from her life, as she freely admits she loves to lie and doesn't care if she's believed. That said, this is the story of a child/God/tube from day one to shortly after her third birthday. From scrupulously debating within herself what should be her third spoken word to finally confessing to her parents she knows how to speak (complex thoughts in complex sentences), Nothomb's little creation bears no resemblance to any infant or toddler in her self-awareness, philosophical musings, angst and refusal to follow developmental milestones, but this is what makes her progress a joy to follow. It takes quite literally those who would claim they are exactly the same person, with the same internal voice, that they were from the moment they achieved consciousness. It also provides an interesting look into the lasting psychological repercussions of WWII on the Japanese. As before, I love her books for giving so much in so little space without being clotted. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10782401206538257183" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText1603757850453445185" style="display:none">In Japanese , the character used for Rain is the same as the character used for Amelie.<br /><br />Amelie is a Belgian who lived her toddler days in Kobe Japan when her diplomat parents were stationed there .This little novella encapsulates her memories from age Two to Three.<br /><br />‘What a load of crock ?’ would be the first response from anyone after reading that statement. <span id="freeTextContainer3415207570026278082">Already the one-line description "an autobiography from age 0-3" had me intrigued, but I wasn't prepared for the amount and level of philosophical thought, not to mention wit, which could be incorporated into what is essentially a 150-page toddler's internal monologue.   <span id="freeText3415207570026278082" style="display:none">Already the one-line description "an autobiography from age 0-3" had me intrigued, but I wasn't prepared for the amount and level of philosophical thought, not to mention wit, which could be incorporated into what is essentially a 150-page toddler's internal monologue. Tweeness is avoided by having the child reason and express herself (in her own mind, if not to others) like an adult (a fairly sophisticated adult at that), which makes the contrast with her infantile emotional states and physical abilites all the more amusing. As a father of a three-year old girl myself, it makes me wonder even more what's going on in my own daughter's head.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer3267269401683857571">Wow--the most original work of fiction in 100 years of fiction. I usually don't review more than "a good beat, and I can dance to it," but this exploration of mind, self, and whether we are part of the world or separate from it makes the old existentialists seem like the children. The narrator/god explains her perception, from birth to age three. Will she have desires, or just be whole? The universe (Japan) embraces her, and those in tune with the universe understand that an act as simple as sav</span>
  <span id="freeText3267269401683857571" style="display:none">Wow--the most original work of fiction in 100 years of fiction. I usually don't review more than "a good beat, and I can dance to it," but this exploration of mind, self, and whether we are part of the world or separate from it makes the old existentialists seem like the children. The narrator/god explains her perception, from birth to age three. Will she have desires, or just be whole? The universe (Japan) embraces her, and those in tune with the universe understand that an act as simple as saving her from drowning would irreparably destroy the natural world.<br /><br />Instantly jumped to my top 5 books ever. </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('BEtXUdVRPkVP0a/tDCgyEW1ti2clkAv3A3lhQdqxUVqY8+BT5rMvkQkC0nXlJz6W2uEQAAqaQx9qmXy+qbEsdA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('flVFcBXLTET5w2pc9ui5xU5YC6tWQrIyaAoRV9BhT6Hi7fJyJildkL8QF8Qf57VC+dSQzHlI+toB6gyoo2Eyjw==')})
  }

Son los años de la junta militar, hay controles de soldados armados y tensión en el ambiente. El hijo se llama Gaspar y el padre trata de protegerlo del destino que le ha sido asignado. La madre murió en circunstancias poco claras, en un accidente que acaso no lo fue.\n\nComo su padre, Gaspar está llamado a ser un médium en una sociedad secreta, la Orden, que contacta con la Oscuridad en busca de la vida eterna mediante atroces rituales. En ellos es vital disponer de un médium, pero el destino de estos seres dotados de poderes especiales es cruel, porque su desgaste físico y mental es rápido e implacable. Los orígenes de la Orden, regida por la poderosa familia de la madre de Gaspar, se remontan a siglos atrás, cuando el conocimiento de la Oscuridad llegó desde el corazón de África a Inglaterra y desde allí se extendió hasta Argentina.\n\nEl lector encontrará en estas páginas casas cuyo interior muta; pasadizos que esconden monstruos inimaginables; rituales con fieros y extáticos sacrificios humanos; andanzas en el Londres psicodélico de los años sesenta, donde la madre de Gaspar conoció a un joven cantante de aire andrógino llamado David; párpados humanos convertidos en fetiches; enigmáticas liturgias sexuales; la relación entre padres e hijos, con la carga de una herencia atroz; y, de fondo, la represión de la dictadura militar, los desaparecidos y, más adelante, la incierta llegada de la democracia, los primeros brotes del sida en Buenos Aires... El terror sobrenatural se entrecruza con terrores muy reales en esta novela perturbadora y deslumbrante, que consagra a Mariana Enriquez como una escritora fundamental de las letras latinoamericanas del siglo XXI.\n\n«Mariana Enriquez es una escritora fascinante que exige ser leída. Como a Bolaño, le interesan las cuestiones de la vida y la muerte; su ficción nos impacta con la fuerza de un tren de mercancías» (Dave Eggers).\n\n«Una narradora oscura, minuciosa, terrible y cautivadora» (Javier Calvo).\n\n«Leer a Enriquez es una experiencia dura, emocionante. Sus historias no son tranquilizantes. Es una maestra de lo macabro que no apela a emociones baratas, sino que cada pequeño detalle de oscuridad está allí por una razón y cada una de sus historias contiene una carga política» (Anne Meadows, Granta).\n\n«Mariana Enriquez muestra todo y lo muestra con morbo. Con veintisiete años de edad, Némirovsky saltó a la fama con esta breve joya literaria sobre la venganza de una adolescente, editada en Francia en 1930 y traducida al castellano en 1986.\n Instalados en un lujoso piso de París, los Kampf poseen todo lo que el dinero puede comprar, excepto lo más difícil: el reconocimiento de la alta sociedad francesa. Así pues, con el propósito de obtener el codiciado premio, preparan un gran baile para doscientos invitados, un magno acontecimiento social que para el señor y la señora Kampf supondrá, respectivamente, una excelente inversión y la soñada apoteosis mundana.Pero en casa de los Kampf no todos comparten el mismo entusiasmo. Herida en su orgullo por la prohibición materna de asistir al ágape, Antoinette, de catorce años, observa con amargura los agitados preparativos del baile y siente que ha llegado la ocasión de enfrentarse a su madre, afirmarse a sí misma y realizar su propia entrada en la edad adulta. Con un breve gesto, tan impulsivo como espontáneo, provoca una situación absurda que culminará en un final dramático y revelador.\n Dotada de una afilada percepción psicológica, Némirovsky condensa en pocas páginas una historia donde la difícil relación madre-hija y el ansia de reconocimiento social se funden con la pasión por la vida y la búsqueda de la felicidad.           $$('div.prototip').each(function(i){i.setStyle({zIndex: 6000})});
        }
      });

      newTip['wrapper'].addClassName('prototipAllowOverflow');

        $('bookCover_62055').observe('prototip:shown', function () {
          $$('div.prototip').each(function (e) {
            if ($('bookCover_62055').hasClassName('ignored')) {
              e.setStyle({'display': 'none'});
              return;
            }
            e.setStyle({'overflow': 'visible'});
          });
        });
      $('bookCover_62055').observe('prototip:hidden', function () {
        $$('span.elementTwo').each(function (e) {
          if (e.getStyle('display') !== 'none') {
            var lessLink = e.next();
            swapContent(lessLink);
          }
        });
      });

//]]>
</script>

</li>
</ul>
</div>

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/40416.Am_lie_Nothomb">About Amélie Nothomb</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/40416.Am_lie_Nothomb"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1457300466p3/40416.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/40416.Am_lie_Nothomb">Amélie Nothomb
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
3,493
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer11102488290994360664">Amélie Nothomb, born Fabienne Claire Nothomb, was born in Etterbeek, Belgium on 9 July 1966, to Belgian diplomats. Although Nothomb claims to have been born in Japan, she actually began living in Japan at the age of two until she was five years old. Subsequently, she lived in China, New York, Bangladesh, Burma, the United Kingdom (Coventry) and Laos.<br/>She is from a distinguished Belgian political fa</span>
  <span id="freeText11102488290994360664" style="display:none">Amélie Nothomb, born Fabienne Claire Nothomb, was born in Etterbeek, Belgium on 9 July 1966, to Belgian diplomats. Although Nothomb claims to have been born in Japan, she actually began living in Japan at the age of two until she was five years old. Subsequently, she lived in China, New York, Bangladesh, Burma, the United Kingdom (Coventry) and Laos.<br />She is from a distinguished Belgian political family; she is notably the grand-niece of Charles-Ferdinand Nothomb, a Belgian foreign minister (1980-1981). Her first novel, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/75518.Hygi_ne_de_l_assassin" title="Hygiène de l'assassin" rel="nofollow noopener">Hygiène de l'assassin</a>, was published in 1992. Since then, she has published approximately one novel per year with a.o. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/75520.Les_Catilinaires" title="Les Catilinaires" rel="nofollow noopener">Les Catilinaires</a> (1995), <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/71458.Stupeur_Et_Tremblements" title="Stupeur Et Tremblements" rel="nofollow noopener">Stupeur Et Tremblements</a> (1999) and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/75519.M_taphysique_des_tubes" title="Métaphysique des tubes" rel="nofollow noopener">Métaphysique des tubes</a> (2000).<br />She has been awarded numerous prizes, including the 1999 Grand Prix du roman de l'Académie française; the Prix René-Fallet; and twice the Prix Alain-Fournier.<br />While in Japan, she attended a local school and learned Japanese. When she was five the family moved to China. "Quitter le Japon fut pour moi un arrachement" ("Leaving Japan was a wrenching separation for me") she writes in Fear and Trembling. Nothomb moved often, and did not live in Europe until she was 17, when she moved to Brussels. There, she reportedly felt as much a stranger as everywhere else. She studied philology at the Université Libre de Bruxelles. After some family tensions, she returned to Japan to work in a big Japanese company in Tokyo. Her experience of this time is told in Fear and Trembling. She has written a romanticized biography (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6476164.Robert_des_noms_propres" title="Robert des noms propres" rel="nofollow noopener">Robert des noms propres</a>) for the French female singer RoBERT in 2002 and during the period 2000-2002 she wrote the lyrics for nine tracks of the same artist. Many ideas inserted in her books come from the conversations she had with an Italian man, from late eighties and during the nineties. She used the French Minitel, while he used the Italian Videotel system, connected with the French one. They never met personally.<br /><br />Source: Wikipedia</span>
  <a data-text-id="11102488290994360664" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
